    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_3007191376" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3007191376" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3007191376?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=portuguese">portuguese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=brazilian-authors">brazilian-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=jealousy">jealousy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3007191376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3443967153346445006">Translated from the Portuguese (Brazilian) the title means ‘Lord Curmudgeon’ or maybe “Sir Grumpy,’ a name his buddies gave him in his old age. He liked the nickname. The introduction tells us that at the time the author was writing the word also meant obstinate, stubborn or wrong-headed. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570668464i/28270374._SX540_.jpg' width='440' height='330' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The main theme is about a man who has a wonderful marriage, a child and a great friendship with another couple, a man who had been his best friend all his life. He often has bouts of jealously about his beautif</span>
  <span id="freeText3443967153346445006" style="display:none">Translated from the Portuguese (Brazilian) the title means ‘Lord Curmudgeon’ or maybe “Sir Grumpy,’ a name his buddies gave him in his old age. He liked the nickname. The introduction tells us that at the time the author was writing the word also meant obstinate, stubborn or wrong-headed. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570668464i/28270374._SX540_.jpg" width="440" height="330" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The main theme is about a man who has a wonderful marriage, a child and a great friendship with another couple, a man who had been his best friend all his life. He often has bouts of jealously about his beautiful wife, but he overcomes that and has a great marriage. That is, until his best friend drowns while swimming in the ocean and he begins to re-think how faithful his wife has been to him. With no hard evidence at all he comes to believe she has been unfaithful to him and he ruins his married life, ending up old, disillusioned and … well a lonely curmudgeon.<br><br>The first third of the book revolves around a dilemma. Before the main character was born, his mother gave birth to a still-born boy. She pledged to God that if he sent her another son she would make him become a priest. The boy and his future wife were neighbors in love at an early age – he was 15, she was 14. So this part of the story centers on how he got his mother to give up her pledge so he could leave the seminary and marry his beloved. <br><br>Dom Casmurro was published in 1899 and reflects literary styles of that era: almost 150 short chapters with descriptive titles such as ‘Visit from Escobar,’ ‘Between Dusk and Dark,’ or ‘On the Veranda.’ The author frequently addresses the reader saying things like ‘I could have told this story in a later chapter…’<br><br>Wikipedia and the introduction tell us Machado's works had a great influence on other Brazilian writers in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. In 1941, the Brazilian Academy of Letters founded in his honor the Prêmio Machado de Assis (Machado de Assis Award), still the most prestigious literary award in Brazil. He lived from 1839-1908. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570668650i/28270379._SY540_.jpg" width="440" height="450" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I enjoyed the story and I liked the writing. The novel moved along and kept my interest all the way through. <br><br>Photo of Rio de Janeiro in the early 1900's from monovisions.com<br>Brazilian postage stamp honoring the author from romanianstudies.org<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="3443967153346445006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer444588656" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12303655296907169416">Dom Casmurro (Realistic trilogy #3), Machado de Assis<br/><br/>Dom Casmurro is an 1899 novel written by Brazilian author Joaquim Maria Machado de Assis. Like The Posthumous Memoirs of Bras Cubas and Quincas Borba, both by Machado de Assis, it is widely regarded as a masterpiece of realist literature. <br/><br/>It is written as a fictional memoir by a distrusting, jealous husband, the narrator, however, is not a reliable conveyor of the story as it is a dark comedy. Dom Casmurro is considered by critic Afranio Couti</span>
  <span id="freeText12303655296907169416" style="display:none">Dom Casmurro (Realistic trilogy #3), Machado de Assis<br /><br />Dom Casmurro is an 1899 novel written by Brazilian author Joaquim Maria Machado de Assis. Like The Posthumous Memoirs of Bras Cubas and Quincas Borba, both by Machado de Assis, it is widely regarded as a masterpiece of realist literature. <br /><br />It is written as a fictional memoir by a distrusting, jealous husband, the narrator, however, is not a reliable conveyor of the story as it is a dark comedy. Dom Casmurro is considered by critic Afranio Coutinho "a true Brazilian masterpiece, and maybe Brazil's greatest representative piece of writing" and "one of the best books ever written in the Portuguese language, if not the best one to date." <br /><br />The author is considered a master of Latin American literature with a unique style of realism.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دوازدهم ماه نوامبر سال 2011 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: دن کاسمورو؛ نویسنده: ماشادو د آسیس؛ مترجم: عبدالله کوثری؛ تهران، نشر نی، 1389، در 347 ص؛ شابک: 9789641851219؛ کتاب از متن انگلیسی برگردان شده؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان برزیلی - سده 19 م<br /><br />دن کاسمورو؛ رمانی نوشته ی «ماشادو د آسیس»؛ نویسنده ی «برزیلی» ست، که برخی آنرا برترین رمان در ادبیات «برزیل» به شمار می‌آورند؛ این کتاب در کنار دو کتاب «خاطرات پس از مرگ براس کوباس»؛ و «کینکاس بوربا»؛ از مهم‌ترین کارهای این نویسنده به شمار می‌آیند؛ «ماشادو د آسیس» این رمان را نخستین بار در سال 1899 میلادی، در «برزیل» به چاپ رساندند. کتاب، یادمانهای خودنگاشت «بنتینیو سانتیاگو»، ملقب به «دن کاسمورو» است؛ راوی در این رمان، شرح زندگی خودش، از دوران کودکی، تا زمان حال آنروزگار را، روایت می‌کند؛ بیشتر رخدادهای رمان مربوط به سال‌های میانی سده ی نوزدهم میلادی هستند؛ که شرح کودکانگی و نوجوانی «دن کاسمورو»، و عشق ایشان به «کاپیتو» و ماجراهای آندو را دربرمی‌گیرد. در فصل‌های پایانی این کتاب، که از یکصد و چهل و هشت فصل کوتاه گرد هم آمده؛ نیز، شرح بزرگسالی و ازدواج «بنتینو سانتیاگو»، تا سال‌های پایانی سده ی نوزده میلادی، روایت می‌شود. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="12303655296907169416" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2855759590" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14466798799644113767">What is most intriguing about Machado de Assis's Dom Casmurro is the way the narrator invites you into his world and the intimate parts of his life, the episodes that he has judged formed who he was later to become. Even as he is relating some of his struggles growing up or providing background on important family members, the tone is one of quiet acceptance, philosophical detachment and even humor. When the reader gets to the last 40 or 50 pages, and realizes everything up to that point has bee</span>
  <span id="freeText14466798799644113767" style="display:none">What is most intriguing about Machado de Assis's Dom Casmurro is the way the narrator invites you into his world and the intimate parts of his life, the episodes that he has judged formed who he was later to become. Even as he is relating some of his struggles growing up or providing background on important family members, the tone is one of quiet acceptance, philosophical detachment and even humor. When the reader gets to the last 40 or 50 pages, and realizes everything up to that point has been written as a set up for the conclusion, Dom Casmurro becomes both remarkable and tragic. This is a gripping novel. I plan to read more of this Brazilian author's work. 4.25 stars</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1763845561" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1763845561" />
    <a title="Lizzy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30575403-lizzy"><img alt="Lizzy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512052362p2/30575403.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1763845561?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lizzy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lizzy" href="/user/show/30575403-lizzy">Lizzy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780195103090" href="/book/show/909745.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=favorites-of-all-times">favorites-of-all-times</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=classics-literay-fiction">classics-literay-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=stars-5">stars-5</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=read-years-ago">read-years-ago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=brazil">brazil</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1763845561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4506180674143502181">I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22458.Machado_de_Assis' title='Machado de Assis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Machado de Assis</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82888.Dom_Casmurro' title='Dom Casmurro by Machado de Assis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dom Casmurro</a> so many years back that if it was not for its splendor I might have forgotten it, but a brief revist was enough to remind me why I fell perilously in love with it. One of Brazil's literature masterpieces without doubt. <br/><br/>Love, jealousy and betrayal are the central themes of Dom Casmurro. If it reminds you of Othello or Madame Bovary, you are not too far off the mark. But, at the same time, it could not be more different. The novel is a memoir told in the firs</span>
  <span id="freeText4506180674143502181" style="display:none">I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22458.Machado_de_Assis" title="Machado de Assis" rel="nofollow noopener">Machado de Assis</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82888.Dom_Casmurro" title="Dom Casmurro by Machado de Assis" rel="nofollow noopener">Dom Casmurro</a> so many years back that if it was not for its splendor I might have forgotten it, but a brief revist was enough to remind me why I fell perilously in love with it. One of Brazil's literature masterpieces without doubt. <br /><br />Love, jealousy and betrayal are the central themes of Dom Casmurro. If it reminds you of Othello or Madame Bovary, you are not too far off the mark. But, at the same time, it could not be more different. The novel is a memoir told in the first person by Bento or Bentinho, aka Dom Casmurro, his story of enduring love affair with Capitu. The title character tells us of his younger self, his love, courtship, and marriage to a memorable and colorful Capitu.<br /><i>
  <blockquote>“Lovers' language, give me an exact and poetic comparison to say what those eyes of Capitu were like. No image comes to mind that doesn't offend against the rules of good style, to say what they were and what they did to me. Undertow eyes? Why not? Undertow. That's the notion that the new expression put in my head. They held some kind of mysterious, active fluid, a force that dragged one in, like the undertow of a wave retreating from the shore on stormy days. So as not to be dragged in, I held onto anything around them, her ears, her arms, her hair spread about her shoulders; but as soon as I returned to the pupils of her eyes again, the wave emerging from them grew towards me, deep and dark, threatening to envelop me, draw me in and swallow me up.”</blockquote>
</i><br />As you have guessed, we get only his point of view, and the reader may well ask <i>‘Did Capitu in fact betray Betinho?’ or ‘How would she defend herself if she was here?’</i> However, we never hear her story, but only Bentinho’s reminiscences. Bentinho love and suspicious nature fuels much of his story, but above all, the mystery is the essence of this masterpiece. Whether Bentinho actually was betrayed is pretty much beside the point. We get all his thoughts as they spew out on the page, as near and familiar as a <i>stream of consciousness</i> as we could imagine for a novel published in 1899. Indeed, <i>Dom Casmurro</i> could have been written yesterday.<br /><br />The narrator goes ahead with his story and retraces himself, forgets his thoughts, lies both to us and himself, and generally confuses everything up in a series of short chapters (such as: ‘The soul is full of mysteries’; ‘Idea without legs and idea without arms ;’ 'Hangover Eyes' and ‘Shake your head, reader’). And the result is magnificent. Yes, Bentinho appears wicked, funny and loves to withhold his secrets just to reveal them when you least expect. Regardless, he leaves you breathless and almost yelling at him <i>'Come on, Bentinho, what did Capitu do to you? Please, tell us!'</i><br /><br />Despite the supposed betrayal, throughout the novel there is no doubt regarding their love,<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"We stood there with heaven in us. Our hands united our nerves, and made of two creatures one-and that one a seraph. Our eyes continued to say infinite things, only the words in our mouths did not attempt to pass our lips; they returned to the heart, silently as they came...."</blockquote>
</i><br />How can a book published in 1899 seem so contemporary in style and content? That is one of Machado de Assis’s merits. He is revealed here as a master novelist, as he shapes, manipulates, unites, betrays and ultimately disbands his small collection of characters with bold ease. At no point does he go too far or explain too much, leaving the reader to hesitate, question and argue with himself, and, in the end, the reader cannot help but feel compassionate towards Bentinho despite his lies or any truths. <br /><br />Highly recommended!<br />--------<br /><i>Note: Read in Portuguese, although there is an English translation; quotes from Goodreads.</i><br /> <br /><b>Some more quotes, for those who read Portuguese:</b><br /><br /><b>*</b><i> “Mas a saudade é isto mesmo; é o passar e repassar das memórias antigas”<br /><br /><b>*</b> “Não podia tirar os olhos daquela criatura de quatorze anos, alta, forte e cheia, apertada em um vestido de chita, meio desbotado. Os cabelos grossos, feitos em duas tranças, com as pontas atadas uma à outra, à moda do tempo, desciam-lhe pelas costas. Morena, olhos claros e grandes, nariz reto e comprido, tinha a boca fina e o queixo largo. As mãos, a despeito de alguns ofícios rudes, eram curadas com amor, não cheiravam a sabões finos nem águas de toucador, mas com água do poço e sabão comum trazia-as sem mácula. Calçava sapatos de duraque, rasos e velhos, a que ela mesma dera alguns pontos.”<br /><br /><b>*</b> “Tinha então pouco mais de dezessete... Aqui devia ser o meio do livro, mas a inexperiência fez-me ir atrás da pena, e chego quase ao fim do papel, com o melhor da narração por dizer. Agora não há mais que levá-la a grandes pernadas, capítulo sobre capítulo, pouca emenda, pouca reflexão, tudo em resumo. Já esta página vale por meses, outras valerão por anos, e assim chegaremos ao fim. Um dos sacrifícios que faço a esta dura necessidade é a análise das minhas emoções dos dezessete anos. Não sei se alguma vez tiveste dezessete anos. Se sim, deves saber que é a idade em que a metade do homem e a metade do menino formam um só curioso. Eu era um curiosíssimo, diria o meu agregado José Dias, e não diria mal. O que essa qualidade superlativa me rendeu não poderia nunca dizê-lo aqui, sem cair no erro que acabo de condenar; a análise das minhas emoções daquele tempo é que entrava no meu plano. Posto que filho do seminário e de minha mãe, sentia já, debaixo do recolhimento casto, uns assomos de petulância e de atrevimento; eram do sangue, mas eram também das moças que na rua ou da janela não me deixavam viver sossegado. Achavam-me lindo, e diziam-mo; algumas queriam mirar de mais perto a minha beleza, e a vaidade é um princípio de corrupção.”<br /> <br /><b>*</b> “Nem eu, nem tu, nem ela, nem qualquer outra pessoa dessa história poderia responder mais, tão certo é que o destino, como todos os dramaturgos, não anuncia as peripécias nem o desfecho. Eles chegam a seu tempo, até que o pano cai, apagam-se as luzes, e os espectadores vão dormir. Nesse gênero há porventura alguma coisa que reformar, e eu proporia, como ensaio, que as peças começassem pelo fim. Otelo mataria a si e a Desdêmona no primeiro ato, os três seguintes seriam dados à ação lenta e decrescente do ciúme, e o último ficaria só com cenas iniciais da ameaça aos turcos, as explicações de Otelo e Desdêmona, e o bom conselho do fino lago: "Mete dinheiro na bolsa". Desta maneira, o espectador, por um lado, acharia no teatro a charada habitual que os periódicos lhe dão, porque os últimos atos explicam o desfecho do primeiro, espécie de conceito, e, por outro lado, ia para a cama com uma boa impressão de ternura e de amor:<br />Ela amou o que me afligira,<br />Eu amei a piedade dela.”<br /></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="4506180674143502181" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_102846497" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102846497" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42508-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368721201p2/42508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/102846497?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/42508-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374523039" href="/book/show/1853207.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102846497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11729833216952571580">Oh, Google you have failed me. I wanted to find a nice list of books written in 1900, or at least in 1899. I failed though. <br/><br/>In 1899 though Nabokov, Hemingway and EB White were all born. <br/><br/>Henry James was somewhere between <i>Turn of the Screw</i> and <i>Wings of the Dove</i> in these years. Mark Twain was still kicking around. <i>The Way of All Flesh</i> was about this time, so was <i>The Awakening</i> by Chopin... I was hoping this list would sound better. But this list will do. <br/><br/>Nothing against any of these authors or their</span>
  <span id="freeText11729833216952571580" style="display:none">Oh, Google you have failed me. I wanted to find a nice list of books written in 1900, or at least in 1899. I failed though. <br /><br />In 1899 though Nabokov, Hemingway and EB White were all born. <br /><br />Henry James was somewhere between <i>Turn of the Screw</i> and <i>Wings of the Dove</i> in these years. Mark Twain was still kicking around. <i>The Way of All Flesh</i> was about this time, so was <i>The Awakening</i> by Chopin... I was hoping this list would sound better. But this list will do. <br /><br />Nothing against any of these authors or their books, but Machado de Assis' <i>Dom Casmurro</i> doesn't feel like any of them. None of those books could be read and be mistaken for anything written in the past 50 years, they are firmly entrenched, like most novels are, in the time they were written. Books of different eras feel different, the language is different, their are formally different. Except for a few anachronisms there is little in <i>Dom Casmurro</i> that feels 'old', to steal what Elizabeth Hardwick writes in her introduction to the book (which I would ignore reading until after you've read the book, really, it's one big fucking spoiler), at a hundred years old this book feels like it could have been written yesterday.<br /><br />Why he isn't one of the Modernist masters is beyond me, well it's not actually, that is a figure of speech. It's because Modernism in literature is still a decade away from beginning to manifest itself. It is because big fucking capital EM Modernist writing is too fucking big and important for it to originate in some literary back water like Rio de Janeiro. It's because one person writing wonderfully beautiful and groundbreaking literature but without others following suit yet doesn't make a decisive break in the history of literature. <br /><br />Other reviews probably deal with the injustice of his obscurity better than I am doing here. <br /><br />The book itself. <br /><br />This is the simple story of a boy who's mom made a promise to god. Let me have a child that lives, and if it's a boy he'll become a priest. Growing up the boy is immersed in all things Catholic, and even plays at performing mass, all until at one point he realizes his best friend, the girl next door, is in love with him and that the feelings are mutual. <br /><br />Told in a 148 chapters in 263 pages the novel is something of a Cubist picture told from the perspective of the boy, now a reclusive old man living in a simulacrum of the house he grew up in. It's like super-condensed, Campbell Soup (but without any Warhol connotations) style Proust, with all of the minutia packed into single words and simple phrases but as affecting as pages of luscious prose. <br /><br />There was this great line that I marked at the moment when the narrator realized that the girl next door had the same feelings for him that he had for her, maybe it was just because it reminded me of Leonard Cohen lyrics, but I liked it:<br /><br /><i>"I, future padre, thus stood before her as before an altar, and one side of her face was the Epistle and the other the Gospel. It only remained to say the new Mass, in a Latin that no one learns, and that is the catholic language of all men."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11729833216952571580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_729166339" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/729166339" />
    <a title="Deepthi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7298095-deepthi"><img alt="Deepthi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436848146p2/7298095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/729166339?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deepthi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deepthi" href="/user/show/7298095-deepthi">Deepthi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374523039" href="/book/show/1853207.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298095-deepthi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer729166339" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12115103928944917966"><b>Why you should read this novel?</b><br/><i>Reasons are not listed in order of importance.</i><br/><br/>1. This novel is written by Señor Machado de Assis, one of the most-loved Brazilian writers (I can understand why!).<br/><br/>2. The narrator is unreliable, and we as readers know how interesting they could be. Saying Bentinho is <i>interesting</i> would be an understatement.<br/> <br/>3. Each chapter starts with a cool title. To name a few: "Idea without legs and idea without arms", "Shake your head, reader", "In which is explained the explained</span>
  <span id="freeText12115103928944917966" style="display:none"><b>Why you should read this novel?</b><br /><i>Reasons are not listed in order of importance.</i><br /><br />1. This novel is written by Señor Machado de Assis, one of the most-loved Brazilian writers (I can understand why!).<br /><br />2. The narrator is unreliable, and we as readers know how interesting they could be. Saying Bentinho is <i>interesting</i> would be an understatement.<br /> <br />3. Each chapter starts with a cool title. To name a few: "Idea without legs and idea without arms", "Shake your head, reader", "In which is explained the explained", "Let us enter the chapter" and "The soul is full of mysteries".<br /><br />4. Brilliant prose. For example:<br />"<i>We stood there with heaven in us. Our hands united our nerves, and made of two creatures one-and that one a seraph. Our eyes continued to say infinite things, only the words in our mouths did not attempt to pass our lips; they returned to the heart, silently as they came....</i>"<br /><br />"<i>Lovers' language, give me an exact and poetic comparison to say what those eyes of Capitu were like. No image comes to mind that doesn't offend against the rules of good style, to say what they were and what they did to me. Undertow eyes? Why not? Undertow. That's the notion that the new expression put in my head. They held some kind of mysterious, active fluid, a force that dragged one in, like the undertow of a wave retreating from the shore on stormy days. So as not to be dragged in, I held onto anything around them, her ears, her arms, her hair spread about her shoulders; but as soon as I returned to the pupils of her eyes again, the wave emerging from them grew towards me, deep and dark, threatening to envelop me, draw me in and swallow me up.</i>"<br /><br />5. There is a chapter called <i>"The opera"</i> where an old Italian tenor explains his theory called "Life is an opera" to Bentinho. This is one my favorite chapters and hence, it gets added to my list.<br /><br />6. Though the subject of this book might seem scandalous (jealousy,adultery,etc.), Assis's humor shines through out the novel. And I like smart-funny novels. Hence, on my list too!<br /><br />7. Interesting characters. To name a few: <br /><i>Jose Dias</i>, a lover of superlatives and a dependent of Bentinho's family<br /><i>Dona Gloria</i>, a god fearing woman and mother of Bentinho <br /><i>Uncle Cosme</i>, who is just Uncle Cosme<br /><i>Escobar</i>, Bentinho's best friend<br /><i>Capitu</i>, a girl with tide-like eyes, with arms better than any woman ever lived or will live, Bentinho's first love and wife, the might-be-might-not-be mother of his child (Now you know why Bentinho is unreliable; the dude is confused and confuses the reader too. But, nonetheless, we as readers turn out to be smarter than him.)<br /><i>Bentinho</i>, the narrator and one of the most weird, emotional, funny, vulnerable and memorable protagonists I ever came across<br /><br />8. The narrator constantly voices out to the reader as "dear reader" or "lovely reader", which I find very amusing. To know that there is atleast one person (better if he/she is fictional)in this world who needs your attention is one of the greatest pleasures in life. Bentinho is quite good at making his reader feel important. <br /><br />So, read it for Bentinho. Listen to the poor guy, you won't be sorry.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12115103928944917966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer103797110" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9215776615425009263"> I do not know how to review this novel. Reading it was like watching a great chess master play, or like when he shows you a great game he has played. Move by move, you watch, mesmerized. Narrated in the first person, he even teases you every now and then: go here and see this beauty; go back several moves, recall the logic of what has happened; no, could this be really the meaning of what went before? But this is not a mystery novel. There is no crime to solve. Or maybe there is, but one can't </span>
  <span id="freeText9215776615425009263" style="display:none"> I do not know how to review this novel. Reading it was like watching a great chess master play, or like when he shows you a great game he has played. Move by move, you watch, mesmerized. Narrated in the first person, he even teases you every now and then: go here and see this beauty; go back several moves, recall the logic of what has happened; no, could this be really the meaning of what went before? But this is not a mystery novel. There is no crime to solve. Or maybe there is, but one can't be sure. Early on, you'll think this is a love story. I am convinced that I saw here the most masterful dramatization of first love, ever, in literature (or at least the literature I had known, so far). But this isn't a love story as love stories go.<br /><br />I can only imagine, if this book will be chosen for book discussion of any of the groups here in goodreads, there could be disagreements that could lead to war, which can only be settled in peace if Machado de Assis resurrects (he died in 1908) or if, magically, his characters in this novel come to life and answer our questions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9215776615425009263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2367492945" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2367492945" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2367492945?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781907970504" href="/book/show/34391370-dom-casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2367492945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12068351303654570252"><i>Dom Casmurro</i> was at first striking to me for its unique setting: Brazil in the mid-19th Century, a place which seems at once familiar and exotic. A place where slavery has not yet been abolished; deeply Catholic, yet modern and European in its sensibilities.<br/><br/>The synopses of this novel tend to focus on the theme of jealousy, but this is not the whole story. For me, the novel is a study of human failings, of our tendency towards self-destruction; the ways in which we are slaves to our nature. There</span>
  <span id="freeText12068351303654570252" style="display:none"><i>Dom Casmurro</i> was at first striking to me for its unique setting: Brazil in the mid-19th Century, a place which seems at once familiar and exotic. A place where slavery has not yet been abolished; deeply Catholic, yet modern and European in its sensibilities.<br /><br />The synopses of this novel tend to focus on the theme of jealousy, but this is not the whole story. For me, the novel is a study of human failings, of our tendency towards self-destruction; the ways in which we are slaves to our nature. There is a kind of amorality, or at least a sense of pessimistic resignation that as humans we inevitably impose on ourselves these subtle tragedies, and though we may feel we can and must rise above them, in fact we cannot help but repeat these same mistakes - we are simply drawn to them by our nature. And it is not that we are evil, only that our passions inherently contain an opposing counterpart – in the case of love, that counterpart is jealousy, which is not love’s antithesis, but is in fact an inseparable component of love itself. <br /><br />Along with our failings we have been given an incredible capacity for rationalisation, an ability to re-frame and reconcile ourselves to any personal wrongdoing. As Bentinho looks back on his life, we are never really sure which of his reminiscences are fact, and which are a product of this rationalisation. But it is the quality of his experience that is salient, rather than the question of truth. Though we cannot confirm it, we suspect that certain pieces of the narrative have been distorted, and we see exemplified in Bentinho that very human trait - the complete inability to sacrifice the ego. We see what fragile and tragic creatures we are, who are unable to face the fact of who we are in reality; who must instead twist the narratives of our lives so that they can compare tolerably when held up against the ideal self, which we believe ourselves, falsely, to embody.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12068351303654570252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_134447791" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/134447791" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/134447791?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374523039" href="/book/show/1853207.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=unreliable-narrators">unreliable-narrators</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=reading-through-history">reading-through-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134447791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5585288917028400549">Dom Casmurro is about a lifelong love affair in which one person betrays the other; the mystery is who has done the betraying. The narrator doubles back on himself, loses track of his thoughts, lies both to us and to himself, and generally mucks everything up in a series of short,sharp chapters with titles like "Let us proceed to the chapter" and "Let us enter the chapter." Machado de Assis is apparently Brazil's best-loved author and an antecedent of the magical realist style, and I'd never hea</span>
  <span id="freeText5585288917028400549" style="display:none">Dom Casmurro is about a lifelong love affair in which one person betrays the other; the mystery is who has done the betraying. The narrator doubles back on himself, loses track of his thoughts, lies both to us and to himself, and generally mucks everything up in a series of short,sharp chapters with titles like "Let us proceed to the chapter" and "Let us enter the chapter." Machado de Assis is apparently Brazil's best-loved author and an antecedent of the magical realist style, and I'd never heard of him until this year. Exciting! And this book is weird and wonderful.<br /><br />Early on, a minor character explains that life is an opera. Not metaphorically. Satan, "a young maestro with a great future," is cast out of the conservatory of heaven after rebelling, but not before stealing a cast-off libretto of God's. He turns it into a full opera and begs God to hear it. At last God relents, but refuses to have it played in heaven; instead, he creates this world as a special stage to hear Satan's composition - which, in the lonely fleshing out, has accidentally lost or distorted some of God's themes. "Indeed in some places the words go to the right and the music to the left...Certain motifs grow wearisome from repetition. There are obscure passages...and there are some who say that this is the beauty of the composition and keeps it from being monotonous." That's cool, right? It's even better in context.<br /><br />The foreword by Elizabeth Hardwick spoils everything. You should basically always read forewords last.<br /><br />Heather suggested that Tolstoy's novella The Kreutzer Sonata would make a good companion read, so I obediently read that next; she was totally right. They go together perfectly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5585288917028400549" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_496083414" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/496083414" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/496083414?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780195103090" href="/book/show/909745.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=unreliable-narrator">unreliable-narrator</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer496083414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4702296206458836157">My arms and my hands were practically shaking yesterday while I was in the last 50 pages of this book. One of those novels with <u>perfect</u> denouement. I immediately sent a text message to my brother (who gave this 5 stars) and our friend (who wants to borrow this book so I had to squeeze this in to my already tight queue of to-be-read books) telling them how beautiful this book was. I am glad I forced myself to read this now. I also told them that I was planning to dislike this book to avenge Rober</span>
  <span id="freeText4702296206458836157" style="display:none">My arms and my hands were practically shaking yesterday while I was in the last 50 pages of this book. One of those novels with <u>perfect</u> denouement. I immediately sent a text message to my brother (who gave this 5 stars) and our friend (who wants to borrow this book so I had to squeeze this in to my already tight queue of to-be-read books) telling them how beautiful this book was. I am glad I forced myself to read this now. I also told them that I was planning to dislike this book to avenge Roberto Bolano's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63032.2666" title="2666 by Roberto Bolaño" rel="nofollow noopener">2666</a> that I rated with 5 stars and excitedly lent it to my brother. He read it and gave it a 1 star. You see, <i>Dom Casmurro,</i> got 5 stars from him and so when I was about to start this, I thought why not do the same to him? But I COULD NOT. This novel is too brilliantly-written especially the last 50 pages. It would have been a big injustice to Machado de Assis if I used him just to appease my hurt feelings for my idol Roberto Bolano. Anyway, there are other 5-star books in my brother's read folder that I am still to read hahaha. <i>May araw ka rin, kuya!</i><br /><br />Why I do I like the last 50 pages? Because I shook in anticipation. The book is told in first person by the BEST unreliable narrator that I've read so far in my almost four years of voracious reading. Prior to this, I thought that the best in my book was the guy in Julian Barnes' 2011 Booker winner, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending" title="The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sense of an Ending</a> (4 stars). This 1899 book by Machado de Assis truly pushed to the edge the meaning of unreliable narrator for me. <b>Bentinho</b> a.k.a., <b>Dom Casmurro</b> is wicked, childish yet funny and he has this habit to withholding his secrets until your hands and arms are shaking in excitement murmuring <i>what now? come on. what's the truth? tell me!</i> <br /><br />But, why am I not giving this a full 5 stars? Take note that I am not giving you any hint on what was revealed in those last 50 pages. That part was the "real deal" in this book and I want you to enjoy it as much as I did. However, the book's composition for me is like this: <blockquote><b>first 1-50:</b> 3 stars (<i>interesting</i>)<br /><b>middle 155 pages:</b> 2 or sometimes 1 star (<i>it's okay to sometimes boring.</i>)<br /><b>last 50 pages:</b> 5 stars (<i>amazing!</i>)</blockquote>You really have to finish the book to appreciate the story. I have a feeling that those who gave this a 1-star rating were those who were not able to bear the arduous and tiresome middle. For those who don't care about spoilers, here is the breakdown for those 155 pages comprising the middle of the book:<blockquote>
  <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<u>50 pages:</u><br />Bentinho: "I don't want to be a padre! (priest)"<br />Dona Juana (the mother): "But you have to."<br />So he went to the seminary but he became a lawyer.<br /><br /><u>50 pages:</u><br />Bentinho: "I want to have a child."<br />Capitu (the wife): *suffering in silence*<br />Then they had a child, Ezekiel.<br /><br /><u>55 pages:</u><br />Bentinho: "Ezekiel is not my child."<br />Capitu (finally, exasperated): "Must you always remain a child, Bentinho?"<br />So I say: Yay! You said it Capitu! Go gurl! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</blockquote>Reading this book is like passing through a long dark tunnel. You appreciate more the bright light at the end of it because of the dark boring path that you want to pass through.<br /><br />I love this book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4702296206458836157" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_636839203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/636839203" />
    <a title="L" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7196445-l"><img alt="L" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578130959p2/7196445.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/636839203?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="L" class="user" itemprop="url" name="L" href="/user/show/7196445-l">L</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374523039" href="/book/show/1853207.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7196445-l?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7196445-l?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer636839203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5809115828138651326">I adored this novel.<br/><br/>First published in 1900, and set in Rio de Janeiro during the latter-half of the 19th century, it follows the fortunes (and misfortunes) of Bento Santiago, otherwise known by his pseudonym of 'Dom Casmurro.' From the outset that pseudonym reveals a lot about our narrator, and the melancholic and suspicious nature which fuels much of his story.<br/><br/>Love ranks highly in this novel, surpassed only by the jealousy which often goes with it, and here Machado appears as a master of the n</span>
  <span id="freeText5809115828138651326" style="display:none">I adored this novel.<br /><br />First published in 1900, and set in Rio de Janeiro during the latter-half of the 19th century, it follows the fortunes (and misfortunes) of Bento Santiago, otherwise known by his pseudonym of 'Dom Casmurro.' From the outset that pseudonym reveals a lot about our narrator, and the melancholic and suspicious nature which fuels much of his story.<br /><br />Love ranks highly in this novel, surpassed only by the jealousy which often goes with it, and here Machado appears as a master of the novelistic form, uniting, betraying and ultimately disbanding his small collection of characters with confident ease. At no point does he go too far or explain too much. On the contrary, throughout the novel he demonstrates a wonderful reserve as storyteller, leaving the reader to doubt, argue, second-guess and, in the end, pity Bentinho and his psychologically-driven narrative.<br /><br />This is a novel which has not received the critical attention and readership that it deserves. Machado's prose is delightful, full of sly humour and a lyrical dialogue which makes the 260 pages of text pass by effortlessly. By the end I felt as though I had spent the afternoon hiding in Bentinho's garden, overhearing his confession or conversation, whichever you prefer.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5809115828138651326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2466784283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14288292477677083529">Remember those days when growing up in a Catholic household the mother always wanted the male child to enter into Seminary School so they could become a priest? After all, mothers know best. And maybe win some celestial favour.<br/><br/>Our stubborn young Bentinho has his path made clear thanks to mom. But like every great story, why not add in a love interest, Capitu. Talk about a monkey wrench. Temptation is too great; temptation is a sin as well.   <span id="freeText14288292477677083529" style="display:none">Remember those days when growing up in a Catholic household the mother always wanted the male child to enter into Seminary School so they could become a priest? After all, mothers know best. And maybe win some celestial favour.<br /><br />Our stubborn young Bentinho has his path made clear thanks to mom. But like every great story, why not add in a love interest, Capitu. Talk about a monkey wrench. Temptation is too great; temptation is a sin as well. Add in SenhorJosé Dias, Tío Cosme and prima Justina for commentary and moral reflection. Add in a good friend Escobar from the Seminary to widen our young Bentinho and Sancha to tempt. Bentinho is a very jealous man; envious of everyone.<br /><br />Thus you have a Brazilian version of a Shakespearean comedy (minus the rhyming couplets). What a true comedy of errors. A real treat to read. Obrigado Susana!<br /><br />Just came across an interesting article via Radio Ambulante.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/14/books/brazil-machado-de-assis.html?utm_source=Newsletter+Radio+Ambulante&amp;utm_campaign=6aa3edc78f-Recomendados_v76_SuperUsers&amp;utm_medium=email&amp;utm_term=0_e2fc316930-6aa3edc78f-94379309">https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/14/bo...</a><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_82702666" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82702666" />
    <a title="Whitaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker"><img alt="Whitaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410926609p2/1415047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82702666?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whitaker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whitaker" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker">Whitaker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780195103090" href="/book/show/909745.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=z_2009-read">z_2009-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=canon">canon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=zz-box-05">zz-box-05</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82702666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5996228735384487993"><blockquote><br/><b>Cuckold</b>: A man whose wife has committed adultery, often regarded as an object of scorn.<br/><br/>O! beware, my lord, of jealousy; <br/>It is the green-ey’d monster which doth mock <br/>The meat it feeds on; that cuckold lives in bliss <br/>Who, certain of his fate, loves not his wronger; <br/>But, O! what damned minutes tells he o’er <br/>Who dotes, yet doubts; suspects, yet soundly loves! <br/>- <i>Othello</i>, Act III Scene iii, lines 91-196<br/><br/>When tears come down like fallin' rain<br/>You'll toss around and call my name <br/>You'll walk the floor the </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText5996228735384487993" style="display:none"><blockquote><br /><b>Cuckold</b>: A man whose wife has committed adultery, often regarded as an object of scorn.<br /><br />O! beware, my lord, of jealousy; <br />It is the green-ey’d monster which doth mock <br />The meat it feeds on; that cuckold lives in bliss <br />Who, certain of his fate, loves not his wronger; <br />But, O! what damned minutes tells he o’er <br />Who dotes, yet doubts; suspects, yet soundly loves! <br />- <i>Othello</i>, Act III Scene iii, lines 91-196<br /><br />When tears come down like fallin' rain<br />You'll toss around and call my name <br />You'll walk the floor the way I do <br />Your cheatin' heart will tell on you.<br />- Hank Williams, <i>Your Cheatin' Heart</i><br /></blockquote><br /><br />The wife whose husband is cheating on her is a tragic victim; the husband whose wife is cheating on him is a comedic one. In the Occidental literary tradition, the cuckold is also a buffoon. How galling it must be then to suffer not only the pain of emotional betrayal, but to suffer also the humiliation of being a laughing stock. What bigger terror can there be then to a tender ego than the threat of being cuckolded? <br /><br />The novel never shows us the supposedly adulterous couple <i>in flagrante delicto</i>. We spend all our time in Bento's head, which might be the only place the delict takes place. Did she? Or didn't she? Is he wearing horns? Or does he only think he does? <br /><br />The genius of <i>Dom Casmurro</i> lies in its depiction of the obsession with this terror. Whether Bento actually wears horns is pretty much beside the point. We get all his thoughts as they spew out on the page, as close to stream of consciousness before stream of consciousness as we can get. Which just might make it the first novel to depict OCD: the sufferer tortured with obsessive thoughts of his greatest fears which he cannot get rid of. <br /><br />Did she? Or didn't she? Who cares? Because it's all the more mind-blowingly great that we never know. For sure, that is. That she did do it. Or didn't. Or did. Or … </span>
  <a data-text-id="5996228735384487993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_912285015" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/912285015" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/912285015?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780195103083" href="/book/show/1770416.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=portuguese">portuguese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-translated">antidote-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2017">r-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-translated">person-of-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer912285015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9013074242166348152">3.5/5<blockquote>
  <i>There might be some exaggeration in this; but that's the way with human discourse, a mixture of the overblown and the undersized, which make up for each other, and in the end level out.</i>
</blockquote>It's fitting that a review of a book whose overarching theme is of fickle memory must also do battle with that particularly slippery titan of an abstract concept. On the one hand, who knows how long it would have taken me to come across this work had the splendorifous skull of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/909746.Epitaph_of_a_Small_Winner' title='Epitaph of a Small Winner by Machado de Assis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Epitaph of a Small Winner</a>, o</span>
  <span id="freeText9013074242166348152" style="display:none">3.5/5<blockquote>
  <i>There might be some exaggeration in this; but that's the way with human discourse, a mixture of the overblown and the undersized, which make up for each other, and in the end level out.</i>
</blockquote>It's fitting that a review of a book whose overarching theme is of fickle memory must also do battle with that particularly slippery titan of an abstract concept. On the one hand, who knows how long it would have taken me to come across this work had the splendorifous skull of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/909746.Epitaph_of_a_Small_Winner" title="Epitaph of a Small Winner by Machado de Assis" rel="nofollow noopener">Epitaph of a Small Winner</a>, or <i>Memórias Póstumas de Brás Cubas</i> if you prefer, not caught my heart. On the other is the double edged sword of inspired completionism, for I gave as much benefit of the doubt to this piece due to its favorable predecessor as I unfavorably compared it to said former and the same. It doesn't help that my average reviewer persona emulates de Assis' narrator to such an incessant degree (albeit with more cursing on my part), so the bias, whichever direction it runs, is there.<br /><br />Whatever the case, unlike EoaSW, this did not prove a favorite. To be fair, I would likely have had to dedicate myself to fin de siècle and older literature for some time before picking this up to appreciate it, as much of the turns and tricks it espouses have become borderline conventional today unless your world is composed solely of <i>Tristram Shandy</i> and epistolary novels, of course). As such, I did not, and despite doing my best to put myself in the mindset of past me encountering de Assis' earlier work, the first of this purported trilogy which DC is the last, I still found myself less than titillated. This can be conjectured from the fact that all the quotes I found worthwhile are nearly pure authorial digression and have little or nothing to do with the text that supposedly birthed them. Admittedly, there's a particular chunk of text that is one of the most brilliant pieces of writing I've ever come across, but as it is another nearly stand alone thought exercise rather than an integral part of gunpowder, treason and plot, I feel comfortable treating with it separately.<br /><br />Somewhere down the line I heard that DC is a text taught in Brazilian schools of the pre-collegiate levels. I haven't bothered to fact check this, but it wouldn't surprise me if it were true, as for all of de Assis' eccentricities, he does in fact deliver a cohesive plot that, as it occurs for the most part in the mind and heart of an imagined fifteen year old boy, would have a strong appeal to that portion of the classroom which usually dominates the discourse. However, despite how much of a theoretical benefit of a doubt the afterword's author insists on giving de Assis', the thematic focus is, stripped of its fripperies, a mere repetition of toxic masculine mundanities, and all the tension promised in the beginning by a sensitive and inquisitive young boy peters out in the solidness of paranoia, possessiveness, and funerals overseas. I could bend over backwards and into that untrustworthy narrator guise, but it'd still be less interesting than the dead nobleman in this work's predecessor.<br /> <br />I'm going to leave off on a positive note, cause seriously, how many black writers writing in translation in the 19th century do you know off the top of your head? Alexandre Dumas is one, Alexander Pushkin another to a degree (what is with all these Alexanders I wonder), and I'm going to have to keep digging from here on out. The foreword even states that de Assis took especial pride in his black ancestry during a time when antiblack slavery still existed in his homeland, which is just fucking fabulous. There's also that God and Satan opera business I referred to, which if I was less tired and more careless about copyrights and such I'd type out right here, but seeing as both characteristics are holding in place, I'll simply let interested parties know that the sequence may be found on the eighteenth through the twentieth pages of this particular edition. Apparently this is an unabridged type, so be careful that your chosen copy hasn't chopped out a redeeming feature or two.<blockquote>
  <i>If you find anything similar in this book, dear reader, let me know, so that I can correct it in the second edition: there's nothing worse than giving the longest of legs to the shortest of ideas.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="9013074242166348152" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_84670720" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84670720" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632452-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539718887p2/632452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84670720?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/632452-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780195103090" href="/book/show/909745.Dom_Casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84670720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6201798298511087506"><b>HEADLINE: Did Escobar and Capitu commit adultery or not? (Answer at the end of this review.)</b><br/><br/><br/><br/><i>Dom Casmurro</i> is a startling book. How can a book published in 1899 seem so contemporary in style and content? I still ponder this. One requires only a bit of scene setting in the Brazil of the 19th Century from some other source in order to get in the swing of this novel very quickly. John Gledson's Foreword to the Library of Latin America's edition does just that. He is also the translator.<br/><br/>This is a fict</span>
  <span id="freeText6201798298511087506" style="display:none"><b>HEADLINE: Did Escobar and Capitu commit adultery or not? (Answer at the end of this review.)</b><br /><br /><br /><br /><i>Dom Casmurro</i> is a startling book. How can a book published in 1899 seem so contemporary in style and content? I still ponder this. One requires only a bit of scene setting in the Brazil of the 19th Century from some other source in order to get in the swing of this novel very quickly. John Gledson's Foreword to the Library of Latin America's edition does just that. He is also the translator.<br /><br />This is a fictional memoir. The memoirist is the title character who writes concerning his younger self of decades earlier, Bento, and Bento's love, courtship, and marriage to a memorable and vivid female character named Capitu. His mother, her dependents, his good friend, Escobar, his son, are supporting characters in his memoir. Dom Casmurro has survived everyone of whom he writes, including, I think, Bento.<br /><br />On the face of it, Dom Casmurro's memoir is straight forward. The problem is that as we read, we become more and more certain that whatever happened in his youth did not happen as he is telling it. This novel is a skillful study of the nature of human memory, the loss of memory, and the invention of memories. We are all proud that we know what an unreliable narrator is. This novel, from beginning to end, is an exhibition of an unreliable narrator like none you have encountered.<br /><br />It is impossible for me to predict your reaction to Dom Casmurro—the narrator, not the novel. You may be repelled or disgusted. You may like him immensely. You may laugh at him. You may become sad for him. He may remind you of someone you detest. He may remind you of yourself. Whatever your reaction, it will be worth your time.<br /><br />And did Capitu and Escobar commit adultery? No, silly. Capitu and Escobar are fictional characters. They do not exist and never did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6201798298511087506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1482803554" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leslie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leslie" href="/user/show/11351200-leslie">Leslie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/24793146-dom-casmurro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11351200-leslie?shelf=south-american">south-american</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1482803554" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16614428559623561465">I took 1/2 star off my rating for this Kindle edition due to formatting problems (such as it not keeping words intact - for example, the word not might have the no at the end of a line and the t at the beginning of the next line!) & there were some strange translation choices as well. I was glad to be able to get this Brazilian classic (in English) for free from <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.ebook.visitbrasil.com'>www.ebook.visitbrasil.com</a> but had to chuckle at times to the blatant tourism propaganda in some of the notes included. For example,<br/><i><br/>"<b>But</b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText16614428559623561465" style="display:none">I took 1/2 star off my rating for this Kindle edition due to formatting problems (such as it not keeping words intact - for example, the word not might have the no at the end of a line and the t at the beginning of the next line!) &amp; there were some strange translation choices as well. I was glad to be able to get this Brazilian classic (in English) for free from <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.ebook.visitbrasil.com">www.ebook.visitbrasil.com</a> but had to chuckle at times to the blatant tourism propaganda in some of the notes included. For example,<br /><i><br />"<b>Butterflies</b><br />Butterflies are one of the most enchanting parts of Brazilian wildlife. In Brazil, there is an impressive variety of 3,500 species, the majority of which are found in the Amazon.<br />    Some zoos (for example, the Brasília Zoo) spread throughout Brazil have butterfly gardens that show some of the species' diversity.<br />    At the butterfly garden in Campos de Jordão, São Paulo, you can learn about the butterflies and walk among them as well. You get the chance to see their delicate flight, the designs on their wings and even their mating rituals."</i><br /><br />Despite the sometimes strange translations (Caputa's eyes were describes as "obliquos" and José Dias is repeatedly referred to as an "aggregate" -- perhaps they meant factor?), Machado de Assis' writing still contained some wonderful images and phrases. One I particularly liked was when Bentinho is describing when he and Caputa first realized that they were in love:<br /><br /><i>"Do not criticize us, unfortunate captain, hearts aren't sailed like the other seas in this world."</i><br /><br />I liked the story (which is quite sad towards the end) &amp; found this an interesting contrast to my beloved English Victorian classics which are from the same time. The basic plot could have been written by Hardy or Gaskell but the flavor would have been completely different.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16614428559623561465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
